codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-width="854" data-height="470" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/eb/WIKITONGUES-_Victor_speaking_Mapudungun.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Victor_speaking_Mapudungun.webm.m3u8" type="application/vnd.apple.mpegurl" data-transcodekey="m3u8" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/eb/WIKITONGUES-_Victor_speaking_Mapudungun.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Victor_speaking_Mapudungun.webm.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="234" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/eb/WIKITONGUES-_Victor_speaking_Mapudungun.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Victor_speaking_Mapudungun.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="352" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/eb/WIKITONGUES-_Victor_speaking_Mapudungun.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Victor_speaking_Mapudungun.webm.360p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="352" /></video></span><figcaption>A Mapudungun speaker.</figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Mapuche</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="/tʃ/: &#39;ch&#39; in &#39;China&#39;">tʃ</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Mapuche and Spanish: <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="es-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">&#91;maˈputʃe&#93;</a></span>, or <b>Mapudungun</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="/ð/: &#39;th&#39; in &#39;this&#39;">ð</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ɡ/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">ɡ</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>);<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HegBer2013_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HegBer2013-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> from <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">mapu</i></span></i> 'land' and <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">dungun</i></span></i> 'speak, speech') is an <a href="/wiki/Araucanian_languages" title="Araucanian languages">Araucanian language</a> related to <a href="/wiki/Huilliche_language" title="Huilliche language">Huilliche</a> spoken in south-central <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> and west-central <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Mapuche" title="Mapuche">Mapuche</a> people (from <i>mapu</i> 'land' and <i>che</i> 'people'). It is also spelled <b>Mapuzugun</b> and <b>Mapudungu</b>. It was formerly known as <b>Araucanian</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-HegBer2013_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HegBer2013-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> the name given to the Mapuche by the Spaniards; the Mapuche avoid it as a remnant of <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Chile" title="Colonial Chile">Spanish colonialism</a>.
</p><p>Mapudungun is not an official language of the countries Chile and Argentina, receiving virtually no government support throughout its history.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino2013_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino2013-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> However, since 2013, Mapuche, along with Spanish, has been granted the status of an official language by the local government of <a href="/wiki/Galvarino,_Chile" title="Galvarino, Chile">Galvarino</a>, one of the many <a href="/wiki/Communes_of_Chile" title="Communes of Chile">Communes of Chile</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It is not used as a language of instruction in either country's educational system despite the Chilean government's commitment to provide full access to education in Mapuche areas in southern Chile. There is an ongoing political debate over which alphabet to use as the standard alphabet of written Mapudungun.
</p><p>In 1982, it was estimated that there were 202,000 Mapuche speakers in Chile, including those that speak the <a href="/wiki/Pehuenche" title="Pehuenche">Pehuenche</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huilliche" class="mw-redirect" title="Huilliche">Huilliche</a> dialects, and another 100,000 speakers in Argentina as of the year 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> However, a 2002 study suggests that only 16% of those who identify as Mapuche speak the language (active speakers) and 18% can only understand it (<a href="/wiki/Passive_speaker_(language)" title="Passive speaker (language)">passive speakers</a>). These figures suggest that the total number of active speakers is about 120,000 and that there are slightly more passive speakers of Mapuche in Chile.<sup id="cite_ref-Zúñiga_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zúñiga-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> As of 2013 only 2.4% of urban speakers and 16% of rural speakers use Mapudungun when speaking with children, and only 3.8% of speakers aged 10–19 years in the south of Chile (the language's stronghold) are "highly competent" in the language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201388_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201388-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Speakers of <a href="/wiki/Chilean_Spanish" title="Chilean Spanish">Chilean Spanish</a> who also speak Mapudungun tend to use more <a href="/wiki/One_(pronoun)" title="One (pronoun)">impersonal pronouns</a> when speaking Spanish.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The language has also influenced the Spanish lexicon within the areas in which it is spoken and has also incorporated <a href="/wiki/Loanwords" class="mw-redirect" title="Loanwords">loanwords</a> from both <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quechuan_languages" title="Quechuan languages">Quechua</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Depending on the alphabet, the sound <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tʃ/</span> is spelled <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;ch&#x27e9;</span> or <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;c&#x27e9;</span>, and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ŋ/</span> as <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;g&#x27e9;</span> or <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;ng&#x27e9;</span>. The language is called either the "speech (<i><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">d/zuŋun</span></i>) of the land (<i><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">mapu</span></i>)"  or the "speech of the people (<i><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">tʃe</span></i>)". An <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;n&#x27e9;</span> may connect the two words. There are thus several ways to write the name of the language:
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mapuche_history" title="Mapuche history">Mapuche history</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Moulian <i>et al.</i> (2015) argue that the <a href="/wiki/Puquina_language" title="Puquina language">Puquina language</a> influenced Mapuche language long before the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moulianetal2015_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moulianetal2015-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The influence of Puquine is thought to be the reason for the existence of Mapuche-Aymara-Quechua <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moulianetal2015_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moulianetal2015-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The following Pre-Incan cognates have been identified by Moulian <i>et al.</i>: sun (<a href="/wiki/Mapudungun_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Mapudungun language">Mapudungun</a>: <i lang="arn">antü</i>, <a href="/wiki/Quechua_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Quechua language">Quechua</a>: <i lang="qu">inti</i>), moon (<a href="/wiki/Mapudungun_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Mapudungun language">Mapudungun</a>: <i lang="arn">küllen</i>, <a href="/wiki/Quechua_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Quechua language">Quechua</a>: <i lang="qu">killa</i>), warlock (<a href="/wiki/Mapudungun_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Mapudungun language">Mapudungun</a>: <i lang="arn">kalku</i>, <a href="/wiki/Quechua_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Quechua language">Quechua</a>: <i lang="qu">kawchu</i>), salt (<a href="/wiki/Mapudungun_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Mapudungun language">Mapudungun</a>: <i lang="arn">chadi</i>, <a href="/wiki/Quechua_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Quechua language">Quechua</a>: <i lang="qu">cachi</i>) and mother (<a href="/wiki/Mapudungun_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Mapudungun language">Mapudungun</a>: <i lang="arn">ñuque</i>, <a href="/wiki/Quechua_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Quechua language">Quechua</a>: <i lang="qu">ñuñu</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Moulianetal2015_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moulianetal2015-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Areal_feature" title="Areal feature">areal</a> linguistic influence may have arrived with a migratory wave arising from the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Tiwanaku_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Tiwanaku empire">Tiwanaku Empire</a> around 1000 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-Moulianetal2015_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moulianetal2015-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a more recent lexical influence from the <a href="/wiki/Quechuan_languages" title="Quechuan languages">Quechuan languages</a> (<i>pataka</i> 'hundred', <i>warangka</i> 'thousand'), associated with the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a>, and from Spanish.
</p><p>As result of Inca rule, there was some Mapudungun–<a href="/wiki/Southern_Quechua" title="Southern Quechua">Imperial Quechua</a> bilingualism among the Mapuches of <a href="/wiki/Aconcagua_River" title="Aconcagua River">Aconcagua Valley</a> at the time of the arrival of the Spanish in the 1530s and 1540s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETéllez200843_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETéllez200843-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The discovery of many <a href="/wiki/Chono_language" title="Chono language">Chono toponyms</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chilo%C3%A9_Archipelago" title="Chiloé Archipelago">Chiloé Archipelago</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Huilliche_language" title="Huilliche language">Huilliche</a>, a language closely related to Mapudungun, has been dominant, suggest that Mapudungun displaced Chono there prior to the arrival of the Spanish in the mid-16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> A theory postulated by chronicler <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jos%C3%A9_P%C3%A9rez_Garc%C3%ADa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="José Pérez García (page does not exist)">José Pérez García</a> holds that the <a href="/wiki/Cunco_people" title="Cunco people">Cuncos</a> settled in <a href="/wiki/Chilo%C3%A9_Island" title="Chiloé Island">Chiloé Island</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">Pre-Hispanic</a> times as consequence of a push from more northern <a href="/wiki/Huilliche_people" title="Huilliche people">Huilliches</a>, who in turn were being displaced by <a href="/wiki/Mapuche" title="Mapuche">Mapuches</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Ramírez "more than a dozen Mapuche – <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_language" title="Rapa Nui language">Rapa Nui</a> <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognates</a> have been described".<sup id="cite_ref-Ramirez2010_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramirez2010-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Among these are the Mapuche/Rapa Nui words <i>toki</i>/<i>toki</i> (axe), <i>kuri</i>/<i>uri</i> (black) and <i>piti</i>/<i>iti</i> (little).<sup id="cite_ref-Ramirez2010_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramirez2010-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Spanish.E2.80.93Mapuche_bilingualism_in_colonial_times"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish–Mapuche_bilingualism_in_colonial_times">Spanish–Mapuche bilingualism in colonial times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Spanish–Mapuche bilingualism in colonial times"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As the 16th and 17th century <a href="/wiki/Central_Chile" title="Central Chile">Central Chile</a> was becoming a <a href="/wiki/Melting_pot" title="Melting pot">melting pot</a> for uprooted indigenous peoples,<sup id="cite_ref-migracioneslocales_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-migracioneslocales-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> it has been argued that Mapuche, Quechua and Spanish coexisted there, with significant bilingualism, during the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Hernadez_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hernadez-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> However the indigenous language that has influenced <a href="/wiki/Chilean_Spanish" title="Chilean Spanish">Chilean Spanish</a> the most is Quechua rather than Mapuche.<sup id="cite_ref-Hernadez_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hernadez-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In colonial times, many Spanish and Mestizos spoke the Mapuche language. For example, in the 17th century, many soldiers at the <a href="/wiki/Valdivian_Fort_System" title="Valdivian Fort System">Valdivian Fort System</a> had some command of Mapuche.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 17th and 18th centuries, most of <a href="/wiki/Chilo%C3%A9_Archipelago" title="Chiloé Archipelago">Chiloé Archipelago</a>'s population was <a href="/wiki/Bilingual" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilingual">bilingual</a>, and according to <a href="/wiki/John_Byron" title="John Byron">John Byron</a>, many Spaniards preferred to use the local <a href="/wiki/Huilliche_language" title="Huilliche language">Huilliche language</a> because they considered it "more beautiful".<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Around the same time, <a href="/wiki/Governorate_of_Chilo%C3%A9" title="Governorate of Chiloé">Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Narciso_de_Santa_Mar%C3%ADa" title="Antonio Narciso de Santa María">Narciso de Santa María</a> complained that Spanish settlers in the islands could not speak Spanish properly, but could speak Veliche, and that this second language was more used.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_decline">Further decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Further decline"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mapudungun was once the main language spoken in central Chile. The sociolinguistic situation of the Mapuche has changed rapidly. Now, nearly all of Mapuche people are bilingual or monolingual in Spanish. The degree of bilingualism depends on the community, participation in Chilean society, and the individual's choice towards the traditional or modern/urban way of life.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeets,_Ineke_2008_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeets,_Ineke_2008-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_and_origin">Classification and origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Classification and origin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mapudungun.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Mapudungun.jpg/220px-Mapudungun.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Mapudungun.jpg/330px-Mapudungun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Mapudungun.jpg/440px-Mapudungun.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1152" /></a><figcaption>Chilean <a href="/wiki/Proverb" title="Proverb">proverb</a> written in Mapuche and <a href="/wiki/Chilean_Spanish" title="Chilean Spanish">Chilean Spanish</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Mapudungun_alphabet" title="Mapudungun alphabet">Mapudungun alphabet</a> used here does not reflect an agreed-upon standard. In fact, there are three distinct alphabets currently used to write the Mapuche language.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>There is no consensus among experts regarding the relation between Mapuche and other indigenous languages of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a><sup id="cite_ref-Fabre_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fabre-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and it is classified as a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>, or more conservatively, an <a href="/wiki/Unclassified_language" title="Unclassified language">unclassified language</a> while researchers await more definitive evidence linking it to other languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Zúñiga_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zúñiga-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The origin of Mapuche is a historically debated topic and hypotheses have changed over time.<sup id="cite_ref-Zúñiga_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zúñiga-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In a 1970 publication, Stark argued that Mapuche is related to <a href="/wiki/Mayan_languages" title="Mayan languages">Mayan languages</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>. The following year, Hamp adopted this same hypothesis. Stark later argued in 1973 that Mapuche descended from a language known as 'Yucha' which is a sister of <a href="/wiki/Proto-Mayan_language" title="Proto-Mayan language">Proto-Mayan language</a> and a predecessor of the <a href="/wiki/Chimuan_languages" title="Chimuan languages">Chimuan languages</a>, which hail from the northern coast of <a href="/wiki/Per%C3%BA" class="mw-redirect" title="Perú">Perú</a>, and Uru-Chipaya (<a href="/wiki/Uru_language" title="Uru language">Uruquilla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chipaya_language" title="Chipaya language">Chipaya</a>) languages, which are spoken by those who currently inhabit the islands of Lake <a href="/wiki/Titicaca" class="mw-redirect" title="Titicaca">Titicaca</a> and peoples living in <a href="/wiki/Oruro_Department" title="Oruro Department">Oruro Department</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, respectively. This hypothesis was later rejected by Campbell in the same year.
</p><p>The research carried out by Mary R. Key in 1978 considered Mapuche to be related to other <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Chile" title="Languages of Chile">languages of Chile</a>: specifically Kawésgar language and Yagán language which were both spoken by nomadic canoer communities from the <a href="/wiki/Zona_Austral" title="Zona Austral">Zona Austral</a> and also with <a href="/wiki/Chonan_languages" title="Chonan languages">Chonan languages</a> of <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a>, some of which are now extinct.  However, according to Key, there is a closer relation still between Mapuche and the <a href="/wiki/Pano-Tacanan_languages" title="Pano-Tacanan languages">Pano-Tacanan languages</a> from Bolivia and Perú, a connection also made by Loos in 1973. Key also argued that there is a link to two Bolivian language isolates: the <a href="/wiki/Chimane_language" title="Chimane language">Mosetén</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yuracar%C3%A9_language" title="Yuracaré language">Yuracaré languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1987, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Greenberg" title="Joseph Greenberg">Joseph Greenberg</a>, a linguist from the United States, proposed a system of classification of the many indigenous languages of the Americas in which the Amerindian language family would include the large majority of languages found on the South American continent, which were formerly grouped in distinct families.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The only families that fell outside of his framework were the <a href="/wiki/Eskimo%E2%80%93Aleut_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Eskimo–Aleut languages">Eskimo–Aleut languages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Na-Dene_languages" title="Na-Dene languages">Na-Dene languages</a>. According to this classification, Mapuche would be considered part of the Andean language family, within the Meridional subgroup which also includes the Kawésgar language, the <a href="/wiki/Puelche_language" title="Puelche language">Puelche language</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tehuelche_language" title="Tehuelche language">Tehuelche language</a> and the Yagán language. To Greenberg, Araucano isn't an individual language, but rather a subgroup composed of four languages: Araucano, Mapuche, Moluche, and Pehuenche.<sup id="cite_ref-Zúñiga_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zúñiga-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> However, the comparative methods employed by Greenberg are controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In 1994, Viegas Barros directly contradicted Greenberg's hypothesis and part of Key's, arguing that a connection between the Merindonal subgroup mentioned above and the Mapuche language does not exist.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Current linguists reject Greenberg's findings due to methodological concerns and opt instead for more conservative methods of classification.<sup id="cite_ref-Zúñiga_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zúñiga-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, many linguists do not accept the existence of an Amerindian language family due to the lack of available information needed to confirm it.
</p><p>Other authorities such as <a href="/wiki/SIL_International" title="SIL International">SIL International</a> classify Mapuche as one of the two languages that form that Araucana family along with Huilliche.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> However, most current linguists maintain a more conservative stance, classifying Mapuche as a language that remains separated from other indigenous languages of South America while its differences and similarities to them are being studied.<sup id="cite_ref-Zúñiga_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zúñiga-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Dialects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table style="border: 1px solid #ccc; vertical-align: middle; width: 248px; margin:0.2em 0 0.2em 1em; float:right; clear:right;">

</p>
</td></tr>

<td class="clade-slabel last">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>


<tr>
<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; border: 0; padding: 0;"><a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">Cladogram</a> showing the closeness of Mapuche dialect sub-groups based on shared features according to Robert A. Croese. Dialect sub-groups are roughly ordered from their geographical distribution from north to south.<sup id="cite_ref-Croese1985_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Croese1985-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Linguist" class="mw-redirect" title="Linguist">Linguist</a> Robert A. Croese divides Mapudungun into eight dialectal sub-groups (I-VIII). Sub-group I is centered in <a href="/wiki/Arauco_Province" title="Arauco Province">Arauco Province</a>, Sub-group II is the dialect of <a href="/wiki/Angol" title="Angol">Angol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Los_%C3%81ngeles,_B%C3%ADo_B%C3%ADo" class="mw-redirect" title="Los Ángeles, Bío Bío">Los Ángeles</a> and the middle and lower <a href="/wiki/B%C3%ADo_B%C3%ADo_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Bío Bío River">Bío Bío River</a>. Sub-group III is centered around <a href="/wiki/Pur%C3%A9n" title="Purén">Purén</a>. In the areas around <a href="/wiki/Lonquimay" title="Lonquimay">Lonquimay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melipeuco" title="Melipeuco">Melipeuco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allip%C3%A9n_River" title="Allipén River">Allipén River</a> dialect sub-group IV is spoken. Sub-group V is spoken at the coast of <a href="/wiki/Araucan%C3%ADa_Region" title="Araucanía Region">Araucanía Region</a> including <a href="/wiki/Queule" title="Queule">Queule</a>, <a href="/wiki/Budi_Lake" title="Budi Lake">Budi Lake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tolt%C3%A9n" title="Toltén">Toltén</a>.
</p><p>Temuco is the epicenter of the Mapuche territory today.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeets,_Ineke_2008_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeets,_Ineke_2008-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Around <a href="/wiki/Temuco" title="Temuco">Temuco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freire,_Chile" title="Freire, Chile">Freire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gorbea,_Chile" title="Gorbea, Chile">Gorbea</a> the sub-group VI is spoken. Group VII is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Valdivia_Province" title="Valdivia Province">Valdivia Province</a> plus <a href="/wiki/Puc%C3%B3n" title="Pucón">Pucón</a> and <a href="/wiki/Curarrehue" title="Curarrehue">Curarrehue</a>. The last "dialect" sub-group is VIII which is the <a href="/wiki/Huilliche_language" title="Huilliche language">Huilliche language</a> spoken from <a href="/wiki/Ranco_Lake" class="mw-redirect" title="Ranco Lake">Lago Ranco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bueno_River" title="Bueno River">Río Bueno</a> to the south and is not mutually intelligible with the other dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-Croese1985_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Croese1985-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These can be grouped in four dialect groups: north, central, south-central and south. These are further divided into eight sub-groups: I and II (northern), III–IV (central), V-VII (south-central) and VIII (southern). The sub-groups III-VII are more closely related to each other than they are to I-II and VIII. Croese finds these relationships as consistent, but not proof, with the theory of <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_the_Mapuche" title="Origin of the Mapuche">origin of the Mapuche</a> proposed by <a href="/wiki/Ricardo_E._Latcham" title="Ricardo E. Latcham">Ricardo E. Latcham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Croese1985_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Croese1985-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mapudungun spoken in the Argentinian provinces of Neuquen and Río Negro is similar to that of the central dialect group in Chile, while the Ranquel (Rankülche) variety spoken in the Argentinian province of La Pampa is closer to the northern dialect group.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201387_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201387-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Grammar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mapuche is a <a href="/wiki/Polysynthetic_language" title="Polysynthetic language">polysynthetic language</a> with noun incorporation and root composition. Broadly speaking this means that words are formed by <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a> <a href="/wiki/Agglutination" title="Agglutination">agglutination</a> of lexical elements to the extent that a single word can require a translation that produces a complete sentence.
</p>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">ex:</div><div><i>Trarimansunparkelayayngu</i></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">trari-</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="surround" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">SURROUND</abbr>-</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">mansun-</p><p style="margin: 0px;">ox-</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">pa-</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="cislocative" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">CIS</abbr>-</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">rke-</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="SURPRISE" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;"><a href="/wiki/Surprise_(emotion)" title="Surprise (emotion)">SURPRISE</a></abbr>-</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">la-</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="negation/negative" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">NEG</abbr>-</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">(y)-</p><p style="margin: 0px;">(<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="epenthetic morpheme" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">E</abbr>)-</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">a-</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="future tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">FUT</abbr>-</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">y-</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="indicative mood" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">IND</abbr>-</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ngu</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="third person, dual" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">3DU</abbr></p></div><p style="display: none;">trari- mansun- pa- rke- la- (y)- a- y- ngu</p><p style="display: none;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="surround" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">SURROUND</abbr>- ox- <abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="cislocative" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">CIS</abbr>- <abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="SURPRISE" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;"><a href="/wiki/Surprise_(emotion)" title="Surprise (emotion)">SURPRISE</a></abbr>- NEG- (E)- FUT- IND- {3DU}</p><p style="clear: left;">'Those two won't yoke the oxen here!'</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prosody">Prosody</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Prosody"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mapudungun has partially predictable, non-contrastive stress and there is no phonemic tone. The stressed syllable is generally the last one if it is closed (<i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">awkán</i></span></i> 'game', <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">tralkán</i></span></i> 'thunder'), and the one before last if the last one is open (<i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">rúka</i></span></i> 'house', <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">lóngko</i></span></i> 'head'). In two-syllable words, for example, when both <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllables</a> are open (ending in a vowel) or both are closed (ending in a consonant), the accent falls on the final syllable. In the case that only one of the two is open, the accent falls on the open syllable.
</p><p><b>Example</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><i>iñ<b>chiñ</b></i> 'we'</dd>
<dd><i><b>nar</b>ki</i> 'cat'</dd>
<dd><i>kü<b>yeṉ</b></i> 'moon'</dd></dl>
<p>With words that have more than two syllables and have the final two either open or closed, the accent falls on the penultimate syllable. If only one of the two is closed, that one receives the accent.
</p><p><b>Example</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><i>wi<b>lli</b>che</i> '<a href="/wiki/Huilliche_language" title="Huilliche language">Huilliche language</a>'</dd>
<dd><i>pichi<b>wen</b>tru</i> 'boy'</dd>
<dd><i>wa<b>rang</b>ka</i> 'thousand'</dd>
<dd><i>mapu<b>dun</b>gun</i> 'Mapuche language'.</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Vowels"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mapudungun_monophthongs_chart.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Mapudungun_monophthongs_chart.svg/250px-Mapudungun_monophthongs_chart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="191" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Mapudungun_monophthongs_chart.svg/375px-Mapudungun_monophthongs_chart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Mapudungun_monophthongs_chart.svg/500px-Mapudungun_monophthongs_chart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="921" data-file-height="702" /></a><figcaption>Stressed <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">monophthongs</a> of Mapudungun, from <a href="#CITEREFSadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino2013">Sadowsky et al. (2013</a>:92)</figcaption></figure>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">

<caption>Vowel phonemes<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201392_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201392-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>
</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Front_vowel" title="Front vowel">Front</a>
</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Central_vowel" title="Central vowel">Central</a>
</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Back_vowel" title="Back vowel">Back</a>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Close_vowel" title="Close vowel">Close</a>
</th>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-front unrounded vowel">ɪ</a></span>
</td>
<td>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-back rounded vowel">ʊ</a></span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Mid_vowel" title="Mid vowel">Mid</a>
</th>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">e</a></span>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close-mid_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid central unrounded vowel">ɘ</a></span>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close-mid back rounded vowel">o</a></span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Open_vowel" title="Open vowel">Open</a>
</th>
<td>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Near-open_central_vowel" title="Near-open central vowel">ɐ</a></span>
</td>
<td>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li>Sadowsky et al. transcribe the vowels with <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa">ɪ, ɘ, ʊ, ë, ö, ɐ̝</span>&#x27e9;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201392_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201392-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Although other sources may follow the traditional transcription <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa">i, ɨ, u, e, o, a</span>&#x27e9;</span>.</li>
<li>In stressed syllables, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɪ,<span class="wrap"> </span>ʊ/</span> are near-close <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-front unrounded vowel">ɪ</a>,<span class="wrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-back rounded vowel">ʊ</a>]</span>, whereas the mid-<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e,<span class="wrap"> </span>o/</span> are centralized close-mid <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">ë</a>,<span class="wrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Close-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close-mid back rounded vowel">ö</a>]</span>. The open vowel is realized as a raised open central <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-open_central_vowel" title="Near-open central vowel">ɐ</a>&#93;</span>, making it sound closer to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid central unrounded vowel">ɜ</a>&#93;</span> than to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">ä</a>&#93;</span>. Unstressed vowels are more close <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">i</a>,<span class="wrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">u</a>,<span class="wrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-front unrounded vowel">ɪ</a>,<span class="wrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-back rounded vowel">ʊ</a>,<span class="wrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Mid_central_vowel" title="Mid central vowel">ə</a>]</span> (though unstressed <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e,<span class="wrap"> </span>o/</span> are still somewhat more open than stressed <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɪ,<span class="wrap"> </span>ʊ/</span>). Utterance-final unstressed vowels are generally devoiced or even <a href="/wiki/Elision" title="Elision">elided</a> when they occur after voiceless consonants, sometimes even after voiced consonants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201392–94_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201392–94-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Traditionally, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɘ/</span> has been described as a close central vowel with an unstressed mid-central allophone. According to Sadowsky et al., the vowel is close-mid <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid central unrounded vowel">ɘ</a>&#93;</span> when stressed and near-close <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-close_central_unrounded_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Near-close central unrounded vowel">ɨ̞</a>&#93;</span> when unstressed, patterning phonetically with the mid-series.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201392–93_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201392–93-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Consonants"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</td>

</td>

</td>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><span style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Lateral_consonant" title="Lateral consonant">lateral</a></span>
</th>
<td>
</td>
<td>(<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_lateral_approximant" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced dental lateral approximant">l̪</a></span>)
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_lateral_approximant" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced alveolar lateral approximant">l</a></span>
</td>
<td>
</td>
<td>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_lateral_approximant" title="Voiced palatal lateral approximant">ʎ</a></span>
</td>
<td>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/m,<span class="wrap"> </span>p/</span> are <a href="/wiki/Bilabial_consonant" title="Bilabial consonant">bilabial</a>, whereas <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/f/</span> is <a href="/wiki/Labiodental_consonant" title="Labiodental consonant">labiodental</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201388_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201388-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The dental series <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/n̪,<span class="wrap"> </span>t̪,<span class="wrap"> </span>l̪/</span> is phonetically <a href="/wiki/Interdental_consonant" title="Interdental consonant">interdental</a> and occurs only in some dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201388–89_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201388–89-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Utterance-final coronal laterals <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l̪,<span class="wrap"> </span>l/</span> may be devoiced and fricated: <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_lateral_fricative" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless alveolar lateral fricative">ɬ̪</a>,<span class="wrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_lateral_fricative" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless alveolar lateral fricative">ɬ</a>]</span>. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201391_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201391-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The plosives may be aspirated. It is often the case with the main allophone of <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/k/</span> (<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_plosive" title="Voiceless velar plosive">k</a>&#93;</span>). Its fronted allophone <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palatal_plosive" title="Voiceless palatal plosive">c</a>&#93;</span> is less frequently aspirated, as is the alveolar <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span>. When it comes to the dental <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t̪/</span> as well as the bilabial <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/p/</span>, aspiration is even rarer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201389_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201389-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> For stops, voicing is not a distinctive feature, as well as aspiration.</li>
<li>Some speakers realize <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʈʂ/</span> as apical postalveolar, either an affricate or an aspirated plosive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201391_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201391-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɻ/</span> has been traditionally classified as an approximant; however, Sadowsky et al. prefer to classify it as a fricative <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_fricative" title="Voiced retroflex fricative">ʐ</a>&#93;</span> as that is the predominant variant in their sample. Other possible variants include a lateral approximant <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_lateral_approximant" title="Voiced retroflex lateral approximant">ɭ</a>&#93;</span> and, in post-nuclear position, a voiceless fricative <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_retroflex_fricative" title="Voiceless retroflex fricative">ʂ</a>&#93;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201390_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201390-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span> may be realized with frication: <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_fricative" title="Voiced palatal fricative">ʝ</a>&#93;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201391_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201391-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Among the velar consonants, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w/</span> is <a href="/wiki/Labialized_velar_consonant" title="Labialized velar consonant">labialized</a>. Before front vowels, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ŋ,<span class="wrap"> </span>k,<span class="wrap"> </span>ɣ/</span> are fronted to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_post-palatal_nasal" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced post-palatal nasal">ŋ˖</a>,<span class="wrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palatal_plosive" title="Voiceless palatal plosive">c</a>,<span class="wrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_fricative" title="Voiced palatal fricative">ɣ˖</a>~<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_fricative" title="Voiced palatal fricative">ʝ</a>]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201388–89_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadowskyPainequeoSalamancaAvelino201388–89-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Orthography"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mapudungun_alphabet" title="Mapudungun alphabet">Mapudungun alphabet</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Aukan_kawin_griffitie_mapuche.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Aukan_kawin_griffitie_mapuche.jpg/240px-Aukan_kawin_griffitie_mapuche.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="180" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Aukan_kawin_griffitie_mapuche.jpg/360px-Aukan_kawin_griffitie_mapuche.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Aukan_kawin_griffitie_mapuche.jpg/480px-Aukan_kawin_griffitie_mapuche.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>Graffiti in Mapudungun meaning "Uprise Meeting".</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Mapuche had no writing system before the Spanish arrived, but the language is now written with the Latin script. Although the <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a> used in this article is based on the <i>Alfabeto Mapuche Unificado</i>, the system used by Chilean linguists and other people in many publications in the language, the competing Ragileo, Nhewenh and Azumchefi systems all have their supporters, and there is still no consensus among authorities, linguists and Mapuche communities. The same word can look very different in each system, with the word for "conversation or story" being written either <i>gvxam</i>, <i>gytram</i>, or <i>ngütram</i>, for example.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microsoft_lawsuit">Microsoft lawsuit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Microsoft lawsuit"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In late 2006, Mapuche leaders threatened to sue <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> when the latter completed a translation of their <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Windows</a> operating system into Mapudungun. They claimed that Microsoft needed permission to do so and had not sought it.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The event can be seen in the light of the greater political struggle concerning the <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">alphabet</a> that should become the standard alphabet of the Mapuche people.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Morphology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>Mapuche is an <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">agglutinative language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeets,_Ineke_2008_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeets,_Ineke_2008-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a> of Mapudungun is flexible, but a <a href="/wiki/Topic%E2%80%93comment" class="mw-redirect" title="Topic–comment">topic–comment</a> construction is common. The subject (agent) of a transitive clause tends to precede the <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verb</a>, and the object tends to follow (A–V–O order); the subject of an intransitive clause tends to follow the verb (V–S order).<sup id="cite_ref-Smeets,_Ineke_2008_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeets,_Ineke_2008-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">Nouns</a> are grouped in two classes, animate and inanimate. For example, <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">pu</i></span></i> is a plural indicator for animate nouns and <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">yuka</i></span></i> as the plural for inanimate nouns. <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">Chi</i></span></i> (or <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">ti</i></span></i>) can be used as a definite animate article, as in <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">chi wentru</i></span></i> 'the man' and <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">chi pu wentru</i></span></i> for 'the men'. The number <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">kiñe</i></span></i> 'one' serves as an <a href="/wiki/Indefinite_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Indefinite article">indefinite article</a>. Subjects and objects use the same case.<sup id="cite_ref-wals_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wals-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Mapudungu uses particles, which is a small group of morphemes that enable the speaker to express how they feel about what they have said. Examples include <i>chi</i> (doubt), <i>am</i> (surprise), <i>nga</i> (regret), <i>llemay</i> (certainty), <i>chemay</i> (amazement), <i>chiam</i> (wonder), <i>amfe</i> (exclamation). There are also more complicated particles such as <i>kay</i>, which suggest the information about to be said is in contrast to what was just said. Another complex particle is <i>may,</i> which is used when the speaker expects to get a positive reaction from what they are saying. One particle, <i>anchi</i>, refers to the subject of the sentence, and an example would be <i>"chem anchi?"</i> which translates to what [is] that (pointed out)?<sup id="cite_ref-Smeets,_Ineke_2008_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeets,_Ineke_2008-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>"An inflection can be added to a noun with -<i>mew</i> or -<i>mu</i>. This <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffix</a> can refer to time, place, cause or comparison.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeets,_Ineke_2008_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeets,_Ineke_2008-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> "An example of this is the sentence</li></ul>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:3em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Mesa-mew</p><p style="margin: 0px;">table-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="locative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">LOC</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">müle-y</p><p style="margin: 0px;">be-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="indicative mood" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">IND</abbr>/<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="third person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">3S</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="subject" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">SBJ</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ti</p><p style="margin: 0px;">the</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">mamüllü</p><p style="margin: 0px;">wood</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ñi</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="possessive marker" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">POSS</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">müle-n</p><p style="margin: 0px;">be-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="nominalizer" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">NOML</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">mi</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="second person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">2S</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="possessive marker" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">POSS</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">tukupu-a-l.</p><p style="margin: 0px;">use-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="nonrealized tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">NRLD</abbr>-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="nominalizer" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">NOML</abbr></p></div><p style="display: none;">Mesa-mew müle-y ti mamüllü ñi müle-n mi tukupu-a-l.</p><p style="display: none;">table-LOC be-IND/3S.SBJ the wood POSS be-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="nominalizer" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">NOML</abbr> 2S.POSS use-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="nonrealized tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">NRLD</abbr>-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="nominalizer" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">NOML</abbr></p><p style="clear: left;">‘On the table is the wood that you should use.’<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<ul><li>Numbers from 1 to 10 are as follows: 1 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">kiñe</i></span></i>, 2 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">epu</i></span></i>, 3 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">küla</i></span></i>, 4 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">meli</i></span></i>, 5 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">kechu</i></span></i>, 6 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">kayu</i></span></i>, 7 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">regle</i></span></i>, 8 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">pura</i></span></i>, 9 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">aylla</i></span></i>, 10 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">mari</i></span></i>; 20 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">epu mari</i></span></i>, 30 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">küla mari</i></span></i>, 110 <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">(kiñe) pataka mari</i></span></i>.  Numbers are extremely regular in formation, which is comparable to <a href="/wiki/Chinese_numerals#Reading_and_transcribing_numbers" title="Chinese numerals">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wolof_language" title="Wolof language">Wolof</a>, or to constructed languages such as <a href="/wiki/Esperanto" title="Esperanto">Esperanto</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronouns">Pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>There are, for <a href="/wiki/Personal_pronoun" title="Personal pronoun">personal pronouns</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Person_(grammar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Person (grammar)">persons</a> and three <a href="/wiki/Number_(grammar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Number (grammar)">numbers</a>:</li></ul>

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Possessive_pronoun" class="mw-redirect" title="Possessive pronoun">Possessive pronouns</a> are related to the personal forms: <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">ñi</i></span></i> 'my; his, her; their', <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">yu</i></span></i> 'our (2)', <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">iñ</i></span></i> 'our (more than 2)'; <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">mi</i></span></i> 'your', <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">mu</i></span></i> 'your (2)', <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">mün</i></span></i> 'your (more than 2)'. They are often found with a particle <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">ta</i></span></i>, which does not seem to add anything specific to the meaning: <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">tami</i></span></i> 'your'.</li>
<li>Interrogative pronouns include <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">iney</i></span></i> 'who', <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">chem</i></span></i> 'what', <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">chumül</i></span></i> 'when', <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">chew</i></span></i> 'where', <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">chum(ngechi)</i></span></i> 'how' and <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">chumngelu</i></span></i> 'why'.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">Verbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Verbs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Most complex verb formations in Mapudungun are constructed with five or six <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Verbs can be finite or non-finite (non-finite endings: <i>-n</i>, <i>-el</i>, <i>-etew</i>, <i>-lu</i>, <i>-am</i>, etc.), are intransitive or transitive and are conjugated according to person (first, second and third), number (singular, dual and plural), voice (active, agentless passive and reflexive-reciprocal, plus two applicatives) and mood (indicative, imperative and subjunctive). In the indicative, the present (zero) and future (<i>-(y)a</i>) tenses are distinguished. There are a number of aspects: the progressive, resultative and habitual are well established; some forms that seem to mark some subtype of perfect are also found. Other verb morphology includes an evidential marker (reportative-mirative), directionals (cislocative, translocative, andative and ambulative, plus an interruptive and continuous action marker) and modal markers (sudden action, faked action, immediate action, etc.). There is productive noun incorporation, and the case can be made for root compounding morphology.</li>
<li>"Spanish loan verbs have generally been adapted into Mapudungu in the third person singular form. An example is the Mapudungu verb for "to be able" is <i>"pwede,"</i> and the Spanish translation for "he can" is <i>"puede."</i><sup id="cite_ref-Smeets,_Ineke_2008_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeets,_Ineke_2008-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>When a third person interacts with a first or second person, the forms are direct (without <i>-e</i>) or inverse (with <i>-e</i>); the speaker has no choice. When two third persons interact, two different forms are available: the direct form (<i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">pefi</i></span></i>) is appropriate when the agent is topical (the central figure in that particular passage). The inverse form (<i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">peenew</i></span></i>) is appropriate when the patient is topical. Thus, <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">chi wentru pefi chi domo</i></span></i> means 'the man saw the woman' while <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">chi wentru peeyew chi domo</i></span></i> means something like 'the man was seen by the woman'. However, that it is not a passive construction; the passive would be <i><span title="Mapudungun-language text"><i lang="arn">chi wentru pengey</i></span></i> 'the man was seen; someone saw the man'. Therefore, a better translation may be 'it was the woman who saw the man' or 'the woman was the one who saw the man'.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_revitalization_efforts">Language revitalization efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Language revitalization efforts"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Chilean Ministry of Education created the Office of Intercultural Bilingual Education in 1996 in an attempt to include indigenous language in education. By 2004, there were still no programs in public schools in Santiago, despite the fact that 50% of the country’s Mapuche population resides in and around the area of Santiago. 30.4% of Mapuche students never graduate eighth grade and they have high rates of poverty. Most language revitalization efforts have been in rural communities and these efforts have been received in different ways by the Mapuche population: Ortiz says some feel that teaching Mapudungu in schools will set their children behind other Chileans, which reveals that their culture has been devalued by the Chilean government for so long that, unfortunately, some Mapuche people have come to see their language as worthless, too, which is a direct and lasting impact of colonization.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
Despite the absence of Mapudungun instruction in public schools, there are limited language course offerings at select Chilean universities, such as <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_Catholic_University_of_Chile" title="Pontifical Catholic University of Chile">Pontifical Catholic University of Chile</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Studies">Studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Studies"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Older_works">Older works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Older works"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The formalization and normalization of Mapudungun was effected by the first Mapudungun grammar published by the Jesuit priest <a href="/wiki/Luis_de_Valdivia" title="Luis de Valdivia">Luis de Valdivia</a> in 1606 (<i>Arte y Gramatica General de la Lengva que Corre en Todo el Reyno de Chile</i>). More important is the <i>Arte de la Lengua General del Reyno de Chile</i> by the Jesuit <a href="/w/index.php?title=Andr%C3%A9s_Fabr%C3%A9s&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Andrés Fabrés (page does not exist)">Andrés Fabrés</a> (1765, Lima) composed of a grammar and dictionary. In 1776 three volumes in Latin were published in <a href="/wiki/Westphalia" title="Westphalia">Westphalia</a> (<i>Chilidúgú sive Res Chilenses</i>) by the German Jesuit <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Havestadt" title="Bernhard Havestadt">Bernhard Havestadt</a>.
</p><p>The work by Febrés was used as a basic preparation from 1810 for missionary priests going into the regions occupied by the Mapuche people. A corrected version was completed in 1846 and a summary, without a dictionary in 1864.
</p><p>A work based on Febrés' book is the <i>Breve Metodo della Lingua Araucana y Dizionario Italo-Araucano e Viceversa</i> by the Italian <a href="/w/index.php?title=Octaviano_de_Niza&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Octaviano de Niza (page does not exist)">Octaviano de Niza</a> in 1888. It was destroyed in a fire at the Convento de San Francisco in <a href="/wiki/Valdivia" title="Valdivia">Valdivia</a> in 1928.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_works">Modern works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mapuche_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Modern works"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most comprehensive works to date are the ones by Augusta (1903, 1916). Salas (1992, 2006) is an introduction for non-specialists, featuring an ethnographic introduction and a valuable text collection as well. Zúñiga (2006) includes a complete grammatical description, a bilingual dictionary, some texts and an audio CD with text recordings (educational material, a traditional folktale and six contemporary poems). Smeets (1989) and Zúñiga (2000) are for specialists only. Fernández-Garay (2005) introduces both the language and the culture. Catrileo (1995) and the dictionaries by Hernández &amp; Ramos are trilingual (Spanish, English and Mapudungun).
</p>
<ul><li><i>Gramática mapuche bilingüe</i>, by <a href="/w/index.php?title=F%C3%A9lix_Jos%C3%A9_de_Augusta&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Félix José de Augusta (page does not exist)">Félix José de Augusta</a>, Santiago, 1903. [1990 reprint by Séneca, Santiago.]</li>

<div class="side-box-text plainlist">Look up <i><b><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Category:Mapudungun_language" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:Category:Mapudungun language">Category:Mapudungun language</a></b></i> in Wiktionary, the free dictionary.</div></div>

<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20031011041028/http://www.xs4all.nl/~rehue/lang/gloss.html">Spanish-Mapudungun glossary</a></li>
